  ⎿  /plan to preview

● The worlds plan is loaded. Here's where things stand.

  What it is: WORLDS_PLAN.md at the repo root is the full design for adding
  world storage to Arche — letting a library define shapes + systems without
  pre-allocating pools, so the caller provides storage and picks compile-time
  sizes (world W { Particle[1000] }, run integrate in W, insert(…) in W).
  Today's global singleton pools become the default ambient world, keeping full
  backward compat. The key leverage is that systems already compile to functions
  over pool pointers, so run … in W mostly just changes which pointer is passed
  — no per-world recompile.

  Verified current status (branch router): only the pre-existing inert
  scaffolding is in place. Nothing functional yet.

  ┌─────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────┐

  │ (from_import)           │                                               │

  with P1–P3 landing before the @cfg gate so shapes are reached through worlds
  before any pool decl can be stripped. The plan file captures the full
  per-phase steps with file:line pointers.

● You've hit a real ergonomics flaw in the plan. Right now the design couples
  two things at every call site — which world (in W) and whether it's test-only
  (@cfg) — so the library's standalone scaffolding ends up double-annotated on
  every run/insert. Let me get your preferred direction before I rewrite the
  plan.

● Verified the crux against the actual code — and it's even cleaner than the
  subagent assumed. Here's what I found.

  How module imports already work: when you #import { lib }, the inliner renames
  every decl to a dotted canonical identity — Particle → lib.Particle — via
  prefixed_dup (lower.c:2185, "%s.%s"), and rewrites all internal references to
  match (hir_rn_decl, lower.c:2320). So inside the imported lib, run integrate;
  and insert(Particle, …); already resolve to lib.Particle — unannotated, 
  context-bound at import time. The shape→storage binding is already
  name-mediated and already context-rewritten. The WORLDS_PLAN was building a
  second, parallel binding system (world struct + registry + GEPs + in W) on top
  of that.

  The simple solution (subagent + my verification):

  - Phase A — qualified pool decls. Let the root write storage for an imported
  shape: lib.Particle[1000]. The parser just needs to accept a dotted name in
  parse_static_decl (parser.c:757) and store "lib.Particle" verbatim. That
  string already equals the inlined shape's canonical name, so
  get_arch_static_capacity matches it and codegen emits @lib.Particle at the
  caller's size — zero codegen changes. Different roots → different sizes. That
  is the "same module, different context, different pool" the user wants.
  - Phase B — import-strip a module's own pool. A lib can keep its own
  Particle[100] so arche test lib works standalone; on import it's dropped (the
  P0 from_import bit, applied only to HIR_STATIC_ARCHETYPE decls) so the
  caller's size wins and there's no double storage. Missing root pool → shape
  falls back to dynamic (cap 0), a safe well-defined fallback.

  What this deletes from the 5-phase plan: the entire world struct + registry +
  in W/GEP machinery (P1/P2/P3 codegen), and @cfg entirely — no per-site
  annotation at all. New syntax: one optional . in a pool decl.

  The one thing this simple design does not give you: two independent pools of 
  the same shape in a single program (true flecs multi-world). It gives one
  caller-sized pool per shape, swappable per program context.

● User declined to answer questions
  ⎿  · The simple design gives one caller-sized pool per shape per program 
     (context-swappable via import), and drops worlds/in-W/@cfg entirely. Does 
     any single program need TWO independent pools of the *same* shape at once? 
     (No — one pool per shape is enough / Yes — need multiple same-shape pools)

  ANY shape from anywhere. lib.Foo is an alias to a canonical shape, that shape 
  ISNT scoped, only the alias.

  check before I say anything.

● This is exactly your model and it's already in the codebase. Let me read the

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● That clears up my mental model — and the key piece is already built.
  canonical_archetype_decl (codegen.c:1031) implements exactly what you said:
  one shape = one pool, a name is an alias, and the canonical decl is the
  first-declared archetype of that shape. canonical_arch_name resolves any alias
  → the one storage symbol, and get_arch_static_capacity (codegen.c:1065)
  already canonicalizes both sides before matching — so a pool declared under
  any alias sizes the shape's single global, reachable from anywhere. Storage is
  not scoped; only names are. So worlds, in W, @cfg, and the whole scoping
  apparatus in WORLDS_PLAN are solving a problem that doesn't exist in your
  model. Good — scrap them.

  With that corrected, the gap shrinks to two concrete things:

  1. Naming a shape you didn't define, in a pool decl. Today parse_static_decl
  (parser.c:757) reads a single bare IDENT, so a caller can't write
  lib.Particle[1000] to size a library's shape. This is the one real syntax gap
  — and because get_arch_static_capacity already canonicalizes, storing the
  dotted string verbatim as archetype_name is enough; codegen needs no change.
  2. Pool ownership / override — the thing your two corrections turn on. Since
  some modules legitimately ship their own pool and some defer to the caller,
  and since two static decls (the lib's Particle[100] and a root's
  lib.Particle[1000]) currently both resolve to the same canonical shape, the
  program needs a defined rule for what happens. Right now
  get_arch_static_capacity just takes the first match in decl order (the inlined
  lib's), silently — no override, no diagnostic.

  So before I rewrite the plan, the one thing I genuinely can't infer is the
  ownership policy. A few coherent rules:

  - Caller overrides — if the root declares lib.Particle[N], it wins over the
  lib's own pool decl (lib's becomes a default/fallback). Lets a lib be
  standalone-testable and re-sized on import, no annotation.
  - Owned is final — a module that declares its own pool owns it; a caller
  trying to re-declare lib.Particle[N] is an error. The lib opts into "caller
  sizes me" by simply omitting the pool (shape stays dynamic until someone sizes
  it).
  - Explicit defer marker — a lib marks a pool as overridable (e.g. a default /
  weak qualifier); unmarked pools are owned-and-final, marked ones the caller
  may override.

  Which ownership model do you want — and when a library wants the caller to
  size its shape, should that be "just omit the pool" or an explicit "this is
  the caller's to fill" marker? That's the fork that decides the whole shape of
  the rewrite.

● Point 1 is already the model: archetypes_same_shape (codegen.c:1012) unifies
  by field-name set, order-independent — so Foo, qux.Foo, and a
  structurally-identical decl all collapse to one canonical shape and one pool.
  Two caveats to flag: it currently matches on field names only (not types), and
  I don't see syntax for an anonymous inline shape literal { bar, baz } used in
  expression/pool position — so "type out the entire shape" interchangeably may
  be a small separate feature. The naming/aliasing half of your model is solid,
  though.

  Now the real one. Your framing — "libs need to be able to do things, but the 
  lib user needs to make the ultimate call" — is a well-trodden design problem:
  ambient resource provisioning with caller override. Here's how the languages
  you gestured at (and others) solve it, mapped onto Arche:

  1. Explicit parameter passing — Zig allocators. Every function needing memory
  takes an Allocator. Ultimate caller control, zero magic — but viral: the param
  rides every signature and call site. This is precisely the in W per-site
  noise you already threw out. Rejected for good reason.

  2. Implicit scoped context — Odin's context (also Jai). A hidden parameter
  threaded through every call automatically, carrying allocator/logger/etc. Lib
  reads context.allocator; the app overrides it for a scope (context.allocator =
  mine; { ... }) and every call inside inherits it, restored on scope exit. No
  per-site annotation, caller has ultimate say, and it's dynamic — the same
  system can run against pool A in one block and pool B in another. Cost: the
  binding is invisible at the call site, and it's a runtime thread, not a
  compile-time fact. This is the right tool only if you need runtime/scoped 
  switching.

  3. ECS Worlds + the Bevy Plugin pattern. The World owns all storage; the app
  creates and sizes it. A library ships as a Plugin — a bundle of component-defs
  + systems that registers into the app's world (app.add_plugins(physics)). The
  lib never owns storage; the app does. The valuable idea here isn't "worlds"
  (you don't need multiple instances) — it's the plugin inversion: lib = a
  passive bundle of defs+systems, app = the authority that instantiates. That
  maps cleanly onto "lib defines shape+systems, never sizes; root sizes."

  4. Weak/default symbols — C linker / __attribute__((weak)). Lib defines a
  default; app overrides by defining a strong symbol; linker picks the strong
  one. This is exactly "lib provides, user makes the ultimate call," with zero 
  call-site annotation and familiar override semantics. Maps to: the lib's
  Particle[100] is a weak default; the root's lib.Particle[1000] overrides it.

  5. Effect handlers / capabilities — Koka, Scala implicits, Rust allocator_api.
  Function declares it needs a capability; an enclosing scope installs the
  handler. Compile-time-checked generalization of Odin's context. Powerful,
  type-safe — but heavy to build in a small C compiler, and more than your
  problem needs.

  Weighing these against your stated constraints — one pool per shape, static, 
  scope-independent storage, no per-site annotation, lib-provides / user-decides
  — the answer is not worlds and not a context system. Those buy you dynamic, 
  multi-instance switching you've said you don't need. What you actually
  described is static override resolution: approaches 4 + 3. The library is a
  Bevy-style plugin (shape-defs + systems + an optional default pool); the
  application has final authority and overrides by declaring its own pool for
  the shape. Resolution is pure lowering-time:

  - A root (from_import==0) pool decl for a canonical shape wins over any
  imported one.
  - Imported pool = a weak default (kept for standalone arche test, overridden
  on import).
  - Neither → shape stays dynamic (cap 0), the safe fallback.

  That's the whole feature: parser accepts lib.Particle[N] (qualified name), the
  from_import bit, and override-aware selection in get_arch_static_capacity +
  the storage-emit site (so you don't double-emit). No in W, no @cfg, no world
  struct, no context thread.

  The Odin-context route stays on the table only if you foresee needing the same
  system to hit different pools at runtime within one program. Everything
  you've said ("single pool per program") says you don't — but that's the one
  judgment call I can't make for you.

  So, the fork that decides the implementation: do you need runtime/scoped pool 
  switching (→ Odin-style context), or is compile-time caller-override enough (→
  weak-default/plugin resolution)? And on override conflicts — when a root
  re-sizes a lib's owned pool, should that be silent (linker-style), or should
  the lib have to mark a pool overridable for the root to be allowed to override
  it?

● Fair — those two statements were loose, and they're about two different
  layers. Here's the precise reconciliation:

  What is implemented — the resolution layer. get_arch_static_capacity
  (codegen.c:1065) runs both the pool decl's name and the query name through
  canonical_arch_name before comparing (line 1070), and find_archetype_decl
  (1047) resolves any alias to the canonical shape. So if a pool decl's
  archetype_name were the string "lib.Particle", the existing code would
  correctly resolve it to the lib's shape and size its one global — no codegen 
  work needed for that. That's the part I meant by "already implemented."

  What is not implemented — the syntax. parse_static_decl (parser.c:760)
  consumes exactly one bare IDENT, then expects [. Given lib.Particle[N] it eats
  lib, hits ., and bails — it never produces a pool decl. So you literally
  cannot write foo.Bar[N] today. The plumbing behind the door is ready; the door
  isn't cut. I stated "implemented" as if it covered the syntax. It doesn't —
  my error.

  How override-aware selection works (concrete). There is exactly one global per
  canonical shape, emitted and sized through get_arch_static_capacity, and
  initialized by the static-decl driver at codegen.c:7040+ (writes
  count/free-count/field-inits into @lib.Particle). The catch: selection today
  is "first matching static decl in AST order," and imported decls are prepended
  during inlining — so right now the lib's pool would win, which is backwards
  from "the user makes the ultimate call." Override-aware means: among all
  static decls resolving to the same canonical shape, pick one winner, 
  preferring the root (from_import==0) over the imported one, and route
  capacity, count, and the init driver through that winner while skipping the
  losers (otherwise both init blocks write into the shared global). Concretely
  it touches three spots: get_arch_static_capacity (1065), get_arch_static_count
  (1087), and a skip-guard in the init driver (7040). Plus the from_import bit
  and the parser change. That's the whole feature.

  Syntax — the root writes a normal pool decl, just under the shape's qualified
  name:

  #import { lib }

  lib.Particle[1000]            // root sizes the lib's shape — wins over lib's
  own pool

  insert(lib.Particle, ...);    // unannotated; resolves to the one canonical
  pool
  run lib.integrate;            // unannotated

  The only genuinely open decision is the override policy — now that you can see
  the mechanism, here's the fork:

❯ who wins if main imports Foo and Bar, they both import and override Baz?      
                                                                                
  Silent override is a no go, you should be able to read the lib without        
  context and understand "this has no storage decisions" or "this storage

● Good — both of those tighten the design in the same direction, and they kill
  the "silent root wins" option outright. Let me answer the diamond first
  because it forces the rule.

  main imports Foo and Bar; both make a real storage decision for Baz's shape.
  Under any "highest authority wins silently" scheme this is exactly the
  ambiguity you'd never want resolved by import order. So the rule has to be: a 
  real pool for a given canonical shape may be declared at most once across the 
  whole program. Two real decls = compile error, naming both sites. There is no
  silent override because there is no override at all — there's one owner, or
  it's an error that tells you to reconcile. main "makes the ultimate call" by
  being the natural single place that owns the real pool; if Foo and Bar both
  insist on owning Baz, that's a loud error, and the fix is that they shouldn't
  have — they should defer (declare nothing) or mark their pool test-only,
  letting main own the one real baz.Thing[N].

  That maps your readability requirement onto the pool decl itself. Reading a
  lib in isolation, every storage line is one of exactly three legible states:

  - no pool decl → "this module makes no storage decisions." The caller sizes
  the shape.
  - test-marked pool (spelling TBD) → "this storage decision is for testing
  only." Kept under this module's own arche test; stripped on import (even under
  the importer's arche test — it's the importee's scaffolding, the from_import
  bit) and stripped under arche build. Never counts toward the one-real-pool
  rule, so it can never collide.
  - plain pool → a real, owned storage decision. Exactly one allowed per shape,
  program-wide; a second is an error.

  So the diamond with test-only pools simply doesn't conflict: both strip on
  import, and main supplies the one real pool. The diamond with real pools is an
  error by construction. No import-order magic, no silent anything, and every
  decision is visible at the line that makes it.

  This also collapses the original plan's whole @cfg apparatus down to a single
  qualifier meaning "testing only" on a pool decl — which is precisely the
  legibility you asked for, nothing more.

  Two things I still need from you before I write this up:

  1. The marker spelling and reach. Is test Particle[4] the right surface (a
  leading test keyword), or do you want the decorator form @test? And does the
  same marker need to apply to a lib's standalone driver (demo :: proc() that
  exercises the test pool), or only to pool decls?
  2. Can a library ever own a real pool for a shape it exports, or is real 
  production storage strictly the application's job (libs may only declare
  test-only pools or none)? You said earlier some modules genuinely need their
  own pool — I read that as a module owning a real pool for its own shape that
  nobody else sizes, which is fine and unambiguous. But for an exported/shared
  shape, "the user makes the ultimate call" implies the lib should not own real
  storage. I want to confirm that line.

  archetypes_same_shape is field-name-only, so once an anonymous literal is
  registered as an archetype decl, it unifies with a structurally-identical

● Explore(Investigate Arche type identity model)
  ⎿  Done (42 tool uses · 61.6k tokens · 2m 24s)
  (ctrl+o to expand)

● Good news first: for archetypes, the problem you're describing is already 
  solved. canonical_archetype_decl matches by the component field-name set,
  independent of the archetype's own (module-prefixed) name — and import doesn't
  rename field names. So ecs.Vec2 {x, y}, rl.Vector2 {x, y}, and a root Vec2 
  {x, y} all collapse to one canonical shape, one %struct, one pool,
  program-wide. Structural unification across modules is exactly what the
  archetype system does. (This is also why Part B — anonymous literals — just
  works once they're registered: same machinery.)

  The gap is non-archetype named types — type aliases (Vec2 :: {x, y}), opaques,
  etc. The subagent confirmed those are nominal and distinct-by-default: a type
  alias interns by its own name (tycheck.c, tyid_of_nominal), tyid_equal is a
  pointer compare on interned IDs, and import renames Vec2 → lib.Vec2 with no 
  structural dedup. So lib.Vec2 and a root Vec2 are genuinely different types
  that don't interoperate. That's your rl.Vector2 vs Vec2 vs ecs.Vec2 pain, and
  it's real and present in Arche today. (Note: tuples, arrays, and proc/func
  signatures are already structural via the intern table; only named
  record/alias/opaque types are nominal.)

  So "fold types into the pool/shape system" splits into three honest options:

  - Model shared types as archetypes. If Vec2 is a component shape, it already
  unifies program-wide for free — zero new work. The constraint: it lives in the
  ECS shape world, and archetype identity ignores field types (names only).
  Good for component-like records; wrong for a nominal scalar wrapper.
  - Make named record types structural too (extend canonical_archetype_decl's
  approach to type aliases). This is the literal "one set of types per program
  by structure." Per the subagent it's a moderate-to-large change spanning
  lowering, the semantic alias registry, and codegen struct emission (~500–1000
  LoC), and it flips the language from nominal to structural for records — with
  the over-unification footgun (two coincidentally-identical types become
  interchangeable). Real philosophical commitment.
  - Program-canonical types, à la pools (the model you're gesturing at). A
  library references a type but doesn't own its canonical definition; the
  program defines it once; structurally-matching defs unify and structural
  mismatches under one name are an error — the same "declared once, no silent
  override" discipline you chose for pools. This is the cleanest fit with
  everything we've decided, and it answers your discoverability worry directly:
  a lib's required types are its interface — the set of type names+shapes a
  consumer must canonically provide, exactly like the set of pools it expects
  sized.

  That discoverability piece — "which types must I define to use this lib" — is
  the same question as "which pools must I size," and it wants the same answer:
  the lib's unsatisfied requirements (shapes with no pool, types with no
  canonical def) are its interface surface, ideally something arche can list.

  Here's my scoping read: this types question is bigger and more load-bearing 
  than the pool + literal-unification plan, and it's independent of it. The plan
  as written (Part A + B) is tight, ready, and doesn't depend on resolving
  types. I'd rather not silently fold a half-formed structural-types redesign
  into an executable plan — that's how the original WORLDS_PLAN got bloated.

  So: do you want me to (a) keep the current plan focused on pools + literal
  unification and capture program-canonical-types as a separate design to work
  next, or (b) hold the plan and treat "one canonical type set per program" as
  the real headline feature to design first — in which case the pool work
  becomes one slice of a larger "libraries declare interfaces, programs provide
  canonical definitions" model?

  Searched for 1 pattern, read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● That changes the answer, and the tuple-group code makes the real problem
  concrete. Here's what actually happens today when every lib defines pos (x, y)
  :: float:

  Tuple groups live in a flat global registry — g_tgroups[64] (lower.c:1320),
  keyed by the bare name pos, and tgroup_lookup (lower.c:1410) returns the first
  match. build_tgroups (lower.c:1391) scans the root file's decls/archetype
  bodies — there's no module loop, and lookup/collapse run on bare names while
  const-decls get renamed to lib.pos at the HIR level. So concretely:

  - No conflict detection. Two libs defining pos differently ((x,y) vs (x,y,z))
  → silent first-wins. That's exactly the silent divergence you've said is a
  no-go.
  - No canonicalization. Identical pos definitions don't merge; they just
  collide by name and the first is used by luck.
  - Hard cap 64, and register_tgroup silently returns past it (lower.c:1327) —
  vocabulary silently dropped.
  - The cross-module story is unhandled — tuple groups are effectively a
  root-file, bare-name mechanism that predates the module rename system.

  So the honest answer to "what happens when every lib defines that?" is: today,
  nothing good — it's the weakest seam in the whole picture, and it's the
  concrete form of your worry.

  Stepping back: everything you've surfaced — caller-sized pools, anonymous
  shape literals, rl.Vector2/Vec2, and now pos (x,y)::float across libs — is one
  problem wearing four hats: shared definitional vocabulary between libraries 
  and the program. And they all want the same three rules, which only archetype
  shapes currently satisfy:

  1. Identical definition across modules → canonicalize to one. (True for
  archetype shapes via canonical_archetype_decl; false for tuple groups and
  named types.)
  2. Same name, different definition → error, never silent first-wins. (False
  everywhere right now — pools take first, tuple groups take first, types just
  namespace-split.)
  3. A lib's used-but-undefined vocabulary is its interface — the set of
  shapes/groups/types a consumer must canonically provide. This is your "clean
  way to understand which types you need to define to use a lib," and it's the
  same question as "which pools must I size."

  That reframes the whole thing. The headline feature isn't "worlds" or even
  "qualified pools" — it's canonical-or-conflict vocabulary, with the program as
  ultimate authority and a discoverable interface of unsatisfied requirements.
  Pools are just the storage instance of it; tuple groups and types are the
  definition instances.

  The plan I have written (qualified pools + literal unification) is a correct,
  ready slice of this — but it's a slice. My recommendation: make this —
  canonical-or-conflict vocabulary + interface discovery — the actual design,
  with the pool/literal work as its first concrete deliverable, and bring tuple
  groups and named types under the same three rules.

  Is that the thing you want me to design — the unified vocabulary model — or do
  you want to keep the executable plan narrow (pools + literals now) and tackle
  tuple-group/type canonicalization as a deliberate second pass? That's the one
  call I need from you before I finalize.

❯ so the crux is that, ideally a single program would have a canonical list of  
  types that every archetype derives from so you don't have things like         
                                                                                
  bar :: int                                                                    
  Particle :: { foo, bar }                                                      
                                                                                
  // Lib                                                                        
  baz :: int                                                                    
                                                                                
  OtherParticle :: { foo, baz }                                                 
                                                                                
  these serve the same purpose but technically have different shapes            

● Yes — that's the canonical component registry, and there's an honest
  constraint at its center that actually defines the whole design:

  You cannot auto-unify bar and baz. Shape identity is field-name based
  (archetypes_same_shape), and that's correct — bar :: int and baz :: int carry
  different meaning despite identical structure, exactly as health :: int and
  score :: int do. If you unified by type, you'd merge every int field in the
  program. So divergence can't be inferred away after the fact. The only way
  Particle {foo, bar} and OtherParticle {foo, baz} become one shape is if both
  libs derive from the same component declaration — i.e. there's a shared
  canonical velocity they both reference, not a bar here and a baz there.

  That's what "a canonical list of types every archetype derives from" means
  concretely: components become first-class, program-global vocabulary —
  declared once, referenced by bare name across all modules (deliberately not
  prefixed to lib.bar the way other decls are), with canonical-or-conflict
  semantics:

  - Same component name from two sources, identical definition → merge to one
  canonical component (no error).
  - Same name, different definition (bar::int vs bar::float, or pos(x,y) vs
  pos(x,y,z)) → compile error naming both sites. Never silent-first-wins.
  - An archetype is a set of references into this registry; two archetypes over
  the same canonical components unify automatically — which is the
  Particle/OtherParticle problem solved at the source rather than after.

  And the tuple-group code is the half-built version of exactly this: g_tgroups
  is already a global, bare-name, unprefixed registry — it just lacks conflict
  detection, lacks cross-module registration, and caps at 64. So this isn't a
  foreign concept bolted on; it's finishing the mechanism the language already 
  reaches for, and extending it from tuple-groups to all components/base-types.

  The bar/baz example then resolves the only way it honestly can: a lib that
  wants interop imports the shared component vocabulary instead of minting its
  own field names, and the components it uses but doesn't define are precisely
  its interface — your "what do I need to define to use this lib," now a
  concrete, listable set.

  So the real design is: a program-global canonical component registry with 
  canonical-or-conflict semantics and discoverable per-lib requirements. Pools
  are the storage slice of it; tuple-groups/types are the definition slice; the
  qualified-pool + literal work I already wrote is one ready deliverable inside
  it.

  This is a real pivot away from "worlds" toward "shared vocabulary." Before I
  rewrite the plan around it — is this the design you want me to commit to paper
  (canonical component registry as the spine, with
  pools/literals/tuple-groups/types as slices under it)? If so I'll restructure
  the plan that way; if you want to push on the model more first, I'm with you.

❯ having a compile error for importing two dlls is not going to work. I like    
  that it's clear but at the same time you can't fix it. That's why I like the  
  doctest pattern for libs for types as well. The libs just need to clearly

● That's the decisive constraint, and it inverts the whole policy correctly: a 
  lib-vs-lib conflict is unfixable, because the person hitting it can't edit 
  either DLL. So the error can't live at the import boundary. The fix is to make
  sure libraries never own canonical definitions that could collide — they
  declare requirements, the program owns the canonical set, and any error
  surfaces between the program and those requirements, which is the one place
  the consumer actually has agency. It's the exact same inversion as pools (lib
  defers storage; consumer provides; doctest fills in locally), now applied to
  types/components. And "the libs just need to clearly surface the types you
  need to maintain" = a library's required vocabulary is its public interface,
  discoverable and consumer-owned.

  That's the design settled. Let me rewrite the plan around this spine.

● Right — that's a necessary distinction I'd collapsed. Not everything a lib
  defines is shared vocabulary. There are two tiers:

  - Private/internal types — owned by the lib, scoped to its #module/#file
  region, namespaced as today (lib.Foo via the existing prefixed_dup rename).
  These can't collide with anything (they're prefixed and scoped), the consumer
  never sees or provides them, and they're the default. This is the normal
  module machinery and it already works.
  - Shared/canonical vocabulary — the deliberate exception: components/types the
  lib operates on that the program must canonically own (unprefixed, global,
  surfaced as requirements). Only these participate in the program-global
  registry from Part 2.

  So Part 2's "unprefixed global canonical" must be opt-in, not the default —
  most of a lib's types stay private and namespaced in their #module/#file
  group; only its interface vocabulary is promoted. That keeps the "everything
  is a group" model intact (private types live in their region) and prevents
  accidental global pollution.

  Your message cut off at "…through…" — you were about to name the mechanism by
  which a lib uses its internal types in #module/#file. Finish that thought,
  because how a lib marks/scopes a type as private-internal versus surfaces it
  as shared-canonical is exactly the boundary Part 2 hinges on. What did you
  have in mind?

✻ Brewed for 2m 35s

❯ what  will this interface syntax look like? is it all docstrings?             

● Not docstrings — and this is worth separating cleanly, because doctests and
  the interface are two different jobs:

  - Doctests are for testing and illustration — they supply local definitions
  and show usage so the lib compiles and arche tests standalone. They're
  examples, not a contract.
  - The interface is the contract: the vocabulary the consumer must canonically
  own. "What do I need to define to use this lib" has to be answerable by
  reading a declaration, not by reverse-engineering an example. If it's buried
  in doctests, it's neither enumerable nor enforceable.

  So the interface should be explicit syntax — and Arche already has the right
  precedent: #foreign { }. That region declares functions the lib expects to
  exist externally (C symbols the linker provides). The interface is just the
  type-level analog of #foreign: a region declaring the shapes/components the
  program must provide. It's a group (matches your model), it's the existing
  idiom rotated from functions to types, and it sits right next to
  #import/#module/#foreign.

  Concretely, a library file would read:

  #requires {
      Particle :: archetype { pos, vel }   // program must own a shape
  satisfying this
      velocity :: vec2                       // program must own this component
  }

  Scratch :: archetype { tmp }   // private/internal — namespaced lib.Scratch,
  never surfaced

  integrate :: sys { ... }       // uses Particle/velocity → bound to the
  program's canonical defs

  // doctest: supplies local Particle/velocity so `arche test` runs standalone

  Reading that, the consumer sees exactly what to maintain (the #requires
  block), the private types stay private (Scratch), and the behavior (integrate)
  is bound at the program against whatever canonical definitions satisfy the
  requirements.

  The one fork worth your call is the shape of a requirement entry inside
  #requires:

  - Exact structural requirement — Particle :: archetype { pos, vel }: the
  program's canonical shape must match (structurally). Clear, but two libs must
  agree exactly.
  - Minimal/structural-subtype requirement — "a shape named Particle with at 
  least pos, vel": the program's Particle may have more fields. More forgiving
  across libs, but looser.
  - Name-only — Particle :: archetype (bodiless, like a forward/extern decl):
  the lib just names what it needs and leaves the whole definition to the
  program. Minimal surface, but the requirement carries no checkable shape.

  Which of those three feels right for a #requires entry? That choice decides
  how strict the consumer-vs-lib match is — and whether #requires is a true
  region or just bodiless extern-style decls.

✻ Churned for 1m 7s

❯ so maybe in this language everything is either a plugin or a driver. Plugins  
  define interfaces, drivers implement them.                                    
                                                                                
  also when a plugin defines it needs foo :: int, the driver should be able to  
  use bar :: int as long as it specifys that bar is an implementation for foo.  

● That's the unlock — "everything is a plugin or a driver: plugins define 
  interfaces, drivers implement them" is the clean spine the whole conversation
  was circling, and the bar implements foo mechanism is the keystone that makes
  it actually work.

  Here's why that mechanism matters: it dissolves the foo/bar/baz divergence
  completely. Plugin A requires foo :: int, plugin B requires baz :: int, the
  driver owns one canonical bar :: int and explicitly binds both requirements
  onto it. The plugins don't have to agree on a name — the driver maps each
  plugin's required name onto its own canonical vocabulary, type-checked at the
  binding (int satisfies int). Unification happens at the driver, explicitly and
  legibly, and it's always fixable because the driver is the thing the consumer
  can edit.

  It also retires the fork I asked about: a requirement is typed but 
  name-mapped, not name-matched. #requires { foo :: int } carries enough to
  type-check the binding; the driver supplies the name correspondence. No rigid
  same-name rule, no silent structural collision.

  So the two new regions, mirroring each other across the plugin/driver boundary
  (and sitting right next to #import/#module/#foreign):

  // plugin — physics.arche : declares its interface, owns nothing canonical
  #requires {
      foo :: int
      Particle :: archetype { pos, foo }
  }
  Scratch :: archetype { tmp }      // private/internal — namespaced, never
  surfaced
  integrate :: sys { /* uses Particle, foo */ }

  // driver — main.arche : owns canonical defs, implements the interface
  #import { physics }
  bar  :: int
  Thing :: archetype { pos, bar }

  #implements physics {
      foo      := bar       // physics.foo is my bar  (type-checked: int ~ int)
      Particle := Thing
  }
  run physics.integrate;     // runs against Thing, via the binding

  A driver component can satisfy many plugins (#implements a { foo := bar },
  #implements b { baz := bar } → both ride the one canonical bar). #foreign is
  the existing precedent — it already declares functions a lib requires
  externally; #requires/#implements is the same idea rotated onto types/shapes.

  I'll fold this plugin/driver spine into the plan now.

● Updated plan
  ⎿  /plan to preview

❯ doctests for plugins need a driver defined to implement the plugin for testing. 

● Yes — that sharpens the symmetry: a plugin declares an interface, so it can't
  be tested as a plugin. Testing a plugin means writing a driver for it, and the
  doctest is that driver — it must define the canonical implementations that
  satisfy the plugin's #requires, bind them with #implements, size the pools,
  and drive the systems. There's no "test a plugin standalone" path that
  bypasses implementing it; the doctest is just the smallest possible driver.
  Let me make that exact in the plan.

     │ Plugins declare interfaces; drivers implement them                      │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Context                                                                 │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Arche's archetype/ECS model has one underlying problem wearing several  │
     │ hats: how do a library and the program that uses it share definitional  │
     │ vocabulary — shapes, components/types, and the storage (pools) for them │
     │ — when the program, not the library, must have the ultimate say, and    │
     │ the two cannot be edited together?                                      │
     │                                                                         │
     │ The original WORLDS_PLAN.md answered with first-class world values,     │
     │ run/insert/delete … in W, world structs/registries/GEPs, and a          │
     │ @cfg(<slug>) conditional-compilation attribute. A long design pass      │
     │ discarded almost all of it for a single spine:                          │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Everything is a plugin or a driver.                                     │
     │ - A plugin (library) defines an interface — the vocabulary it requires  │
     │ (#requires) and the behavior it provides (systems). It owns no          │
     │ canonical definitions and no storage, so two plugins can never collide. │
     │ - A driver (the program/consumer) implements interfaces — it owns the   │
     │ canonical definitions and storage, and binds its own vocabulary to each │
     │ plugin's requirements (#implements { foo := bar }), sizes pools, and    │
     │ runs systems. The top-level program is a driver; a module can also      │
     │ drive its own sub-plugins.                                              │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Why this shape (each point decided during design, grounded in code):    │
     │                                                                         │
     │ 1. Storage is already canonical-shape-keyed and scope-independent.      │
     │ canonical_archetype_decl / canonical_arch_name (codegen.c:1031-1062)    │
     │ make a name an alias for a shape; get_arch_static_capacity              │
     │ (codegen.c:1065) canonicalizes both sides. No worlds/in W/GEP machinery │
     │ — the only storage gap is naming a shape that lives in another module.  │
     │ 2. @cfg is the wrong primitive — a per-decl attribute clashing with the │
     │ "everything is a group/region" architecture                             │
     │ (#import/#module/#file/#foreign). Its only motivation, test             │
     │ scaffolding, is better served by doctests.                              │
     │ 3. Test scaffolding is already a group: the in-file doctest driver.     │
     │ synthesize() (doctest_run.c:78) prepends the file's entire source and   │
     │ splices an example with its own main at top level (doctest_run.c:84);   │
     │ doctests run only under arche test (cli/cmd_test.c:33), never under     │
     │ arche build. A plugin cannot be tested as a plugin — testing it means   │
     │ implementing it, so a plugin's doctest is a driver: it defines the      │
     │ canonical types that satisfy the plugin's #requires, binds them with    │
     │ #implements, sizes the pools, and drives the systems. The doctest is    │
     │ just the smallest possible driver, excluded from any real driver's      │
     │ build.                                                                  │
     │ 4. Conflicts must be fixable, so plugins own no canonical definitions.  │
     │ A lib-vs-lib clash (two DLLs both defining bar :: int differently) is   │
     │ unfixable by the consumer, so it must never arise. Plugins declare      │
     │ requirements; the driver owns canonical defs and the bindings; every    │
     │ error surfaces at the driver, where the consumer can act.               │
     │ 5. Shape/field identity is name-based, and that is correct. bar :: int  │
     │ and baz :: int mean different things; you cannot auto-merge by type     │
     │ without merging health and score. Divergence is resolved not by         │
     │ inference but by the driver's explicit binding: it maps each plugin's   │
     │ required name onto one canonical component (foo := bar, baz := bar),    │
     │ type-checked at the binding. Names need not coincide across plugins.    │
     │ 6. Private/internal types stay private by default. A plugin's internal  │
     │ types are normal decls, namespaced to their #module/#file region        │
     │ (lib.Scratch via prefixed_dup), never surfaced and never collidable.    │
     │ Only what a plugin lists in #requires is shared/driver-owned            │
     │ vocabulary. The global-canonical behavior is opt-in (the interface),    │
     │ not the default.                                                        │

     │ plugin's requirement names to the driver's canonical defs, type-checked │
     │ at the bind. Produces a per-plugin name-substitution applied when the   │
     │ plugin's systems/shapes are lowered into the driver — conceptually an   │
     │ extension of the module qualify/rename pass (hir_q_decl/hir_rn_decl,    │
     │ lower.c:2320-2563), which already rewrites cross-module names; here it  │
     │ rewrites required names → bound canonical names.                        │
     │ - Private-by-default: unchanged module prefixing; only #requires        │
     │ entries are shared. No change to existing decls.                        │
     │ - Doctest-for-interfaces: a plugin's doctest is a driver — it must      │
     │ define canonical types satisfying the plugin's #requires, bind them     │
     │ with #implements <plugin> { … }, size the pools, and drive the systems. │
     │ So synthesize()'s spliced example must accept #requires-satisfying      │

     │ - Unsatisfied/incompatible requirements → driver-side error (reuse the  │

     │ fixable in the driver.                                                  │

● Both good points. Let me check how Arche's regions actually delimit in banner
  (non-block) form so my #requires syntax matches the existing convention rather
  than inventing one.

  Searched for 1 pattern, read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● Confirmed in the parser: banner regions are "sticky to end of file"
  (parser.c:935) — #foreign/#module/#file without braces claim the rest of the
  file. So a banner #requires is idiomatic, and it implies the interface lives
  in its own region/file (which fits module-as-folder nicely):

  // physics/interface.arche  — banner claims the file
  #requires
  foo :: int
  Particle :: archetype { pos, foo }

  // physics/systems.arche
  integrate :: sys { /* uses Particle, foo */ }

  For the binding, the EOF-stickiness actually argues against making #implements
  a region: a driver that implements two plugins couldn't have two EOF-sticky
  banners in one file. And your own phrasing — "specify that bar is an
  implementation for foo" — is naturally per-declaration, no region at all:

  // driver
  #import { physics }
  bar  :: int                              implements physics.foo
  Thing :: archetype { pos, bar }          implements physics.Particle
  run physics.integrate;                   // runs against Thing via the binding

  That keeps #requires as the only new region (a banner, never a block), and the
  binding becomes a small clause on the driver's own canonical decls — local,
  multi-plugin-friendly, and exactly "bar implements foo." (If you'd rather
  group the bindings, the banner #implements physics form is available, but it's
  EOF-sticky → one plugin per file.)

  On the CLI: yes, and the interface being machine-readable is what makes it
  cheap. Two commands —
  - arche generate driver <plugin>: reads the plugin's #requires and emits a
  driver skeleton — stub canonical defs, … implements <plugin>.<req> clauses for
  every requirement, pool-size stubs, and a main calling the systems. This is
  the "what must I define to use this lib," generated.
  - arche generate plugin <name>: scaffolds a plugin folder — an #requires
  interface file, a systems file, and a doctest driver stub.

  Let me fold the banner #requires, the per-decl implements clause, and the
  generate commands into the plan.

● Both points are in the plan now:
  - #requires is a banner region, never a block (sticky-to-EOF like
  #foreign/#module), so the interface lives in its own file — and the binding is
  a per-decl bar :: int implements physics.foo clause rather than a region,
  which sidesteps the EOF-stickiness and matches your "bar implements foo"
  phrasing exactly.
  - Part 3 adds arche generate — generate driver <plugin> reads a plugin's
  #requires and emits a stub driver (canonical defs + implements clauses + pool
  sizes + main), and generate plugin <name> scaffolds a plugin folder. It
  depends on Part 2's grammar and lands after it.

     │ own region/file (fits module-as-folder).                                │
     │ - The binding is a per-decl … implements <plugin>.<req> clause, not a   │
     │ region. EOF-sticky banners can't host two plugins per file, and "bar    │
     │ implements foo" is inherently per-declaration. (A grouped banner        │
     │ #implements <plugin> is the alternative, at one-plugin-per-file cost.)  │
     │ - A requirement is typed but name-mapped, not name-matched — #requires  │
     │ carries enough type to check the bind; the driver supplies the name     │
     │ correspondence.                                                         │
     │ - One canonical decl can satisfy many plugins (bar :: int implements    │
     │ a.foo and separately implements b.baz).                                 │
     │ - #foreign is the existing precedent (it declares functions a lib       │
     │ requires externally); #requires rotates that onto types/shapes.         │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Standing constraints                                                    │
     │                                                                         │
     │ - NEVER move anything to known_failures unless explicitly told. Never   │
     │ touch git. Run make format before tests. Tests under tests/, never      │
     │ tmp/. One test at a time; negative tests for bugs.                      │
     │ - Regression gate: tests/unit/language/systems/ must stay green.        │
     │                                                                         │
     │ ---                                                                     │
     │ Part 1 — Ready to build: caller-sized pools + literal unification       │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Concrete, file-level, independent — the first deliverables of the       │
     │ spine; they stand regardless of how the interface regions land.         │
     │                                                                         │
     │ 1a. Qualified pool decls (driver sizes an imported shape)               │
     │                                                                         │
     │ - Syntax: lib.Particle[1000] (full [C](N){V} grammar), naming an        │
     │ imported shape.                                                         │
     │ - Back end already handles it: import renames a shape to the dotted     │
     │ canonical identity lib.Particle (lower.c prefixed_dup, "%s.%s"); a pool │
     │ decl whose archetype_name is "lib.Particle" resolves via                │
     │ canonical_arch_name and sizes its one global — no codegen change on the │
     │ happy path.                                                             │
     │ - Gap: parse_static_decl (parser.c:757-803) consumes one bare IDENT     │
     │ (line 760) then expects [; lib.Particle[N] bails at the ..              │
     │ - Changes: (1) parser accepts an optional .IDENT chain after the        │
     │ leading IDENT, recording the joined name; (2) lowering carries the      │
     │ dotted name verbatim into archetype_name (confirm hir_q_decl,           │
     │ lower.c:2563, needs no rewrite — it already equals the canonical        │
     │ identity); (3) no-silent-conflict diagnostic in                         │
     │ get_arch_static_capacity (codegen.c:1065) when two pool decls resolve   │
     │ to one canonical shape.                                                 │
     │                                                                         │
     │ 1b. Anonymous shape literals unify with named shapes                    │
     │                                                                         │
     │ - Goal: arche { foo, bar } lowers to the same canonical shape as a      │
     │ named Foo :: arche { foo, bar }.                                        │
     │ - Current: literals parse (SN_ARCH_EXPR, parser.c:1170); in a binding   │
     │ they lower via lower_archetype_from (lower.c:1666), but an anonymous    │
     │ one in expression position falls through to a placeholder               │
     │ (lower.c:770-775) and never reaches canonical_archetype_decl.           │
     │ - Change: replace the fallthrough so an anonymous SN_ARCH_EXPR mints a  │
     │ synthetic HirArchetypeDecl (deterministic name from the sorted          │
     │ field-name set, so identical literals dedupe), registers it in the      │
     │ program decls, and yields a shape reference — existing field-name       │
     │ canonicalization then folds it onto any structurally-identical named    │
     │ shape. No identity-rule change.                                         │
     │                                                                         │
     │ ---                                                                     │
     │ Part 2 — Design-stage: the plugin/driver interface system               │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Policy settled (above); mechanics need a focused detailing pass before  │
     │ coding. Captured with code touchpoints:                                 │
     │ - #requires (plugin): a banner region (no block) beside #foreign — a    │
     │ new #requires marker token + SN_REGION handling in parse_region         │
     │ (parser.c:901), sticky-to-EOF like the others. Decls under it are       │
     │ bodiless/typed entries marked "required, not owned" (components foo ::  │
     │ int, shapes Particle :: archetype { … }).                               │
     │ - … implements <plugin>.<req> clause (driver): a per-decl binding on    │
     │ the driver's own canonical decl, type-checked at the bind. Parser:      │
     │ accept an optional implements <qualified-name> tail on a decl. It       │
     │ produces a per-plugin name-substitution applied when the plugin's       │
     │ systems/shapes are lowered into the driver — conceptually an extension  │
     │ of the module qualify/rename pass (hir_q_decl/hir_rn_decl,              │
     │ lower.c:2320-2563), which already rewrites cross-module names; here it  │
     │ rewrites required names → bound canonical names. (Grouped banner        │
     │ #implements <plugin> is the alternative form.)                          │
     │ - Private-by-default: unchanged module prefixing; only #requires        │
     │ entries are shared. No change to existing decls.                        │
     │ - Doctest-for-interfaces: a plugin's doctest is a driver — it must      │
     │ define canonical types satisfying the plugin's #requires, bind them     │
     │ with #implements <plugin> { … }, size the pools, and drive the systems. │
     │ So synthesize()'s spliced example must accept #requires-satisfying      │
     │ defs and #implements/pool decls at top level (it already splices at top │
     │ level verbatim, so the new region kinds just need to be valid there).   │
     │ No "test a plugin without implementing it" path exists.                 │
     │ - Unsatisfied/incompatible requirements → driver-side error (reuse the  │
     │ sem diagnostic table), naming the requirement and the plugin — always   │
     │ fixable in the driver.                                                  │
     │ - Entry point: the tuple-group registry is the half-built version of    │
     │ shared vocabulary — g_tgroups[64] (lower.c:1320) is already             │
     │ global/bare-name/unprefixed but lacks conflict handling, cross-module   │
     │ registration (build_tgroups, lower.c:1391, scans only the root), and    │
     │ caps at 64 (register_tgroup, lower.c:1327). It folds into the component │
     │ side of #requires/#implements.                                          │
     │ - Before coding Part 2: write a follow-up pinning the #requires entry   │
     │ grammar, the implements-clause binding/substitution lowering, the       │
     │ component-vs-named-type boundary, same-name auto-bind vs                │
     │ always-explicit, and how a plugin typechecks when built only in a       │
     │ driver's context.                                                       │
     │                                                                         │
     │ ---                                                                     │
     │ Part 3 — Tooling: arche generate (driver/plugin scaffolding)            │
     │                                                                         │
     │ The interface being machine-readable is what makes scaffolding cheap,   │
     │ and it operationalizes "what must I define to use this lib." A new CLI  │
     │ command beside cmd_test/cmd_build (cli/cmd_generate.c, registered like  │
     │ the others):                                                            │
     │ - arche generate driver <plugin> — parse the plugin's #requires, emit a │
     │ driver skeleton: stub canonical defs for each required                  │
     │ component/shape, a … implements <plugin>.<req> clause on each,          │
     │ pool-size stubs, and a main that calls the plugin's systems. The        │
     │ generated driver is the consumer's starting point.                      │
     │ - arche generate plugin <name> — scaffold a plugin folder: an #requires │
     │ interface file, a systems file, and a doctest driver stub that          │
     │ implements the interface for arche test.                                │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Depends on Part 2's #requires/implements grammar; lands after it.       │
     │                                                                         │
     │ ---                                                                     │
     │ Explicitly dropped from WORLDS_PLAN                                     │
     │                                                                         │
     │ - world decls, run/insert/delete … in W, world struct/registry/GEPs.    │
     │ - @cfg(<slug>) and flag-dependent compilation — replaced by doctest     │
     │ drivers.                                                                │
     │ - The from_import origin bit — deferring plugins declare no             │
     │ pools/canonical types; test drivers are doctest examples excluded from  │
     │ builds.                                                                 │
     │ - Lib-vs-lib import-time conflict errors — unfixable; replaced by       │
     │ driver-owned canonical defs + explicit #implements bindings.            │
     │ - Inert HIR_DECL_WORLD / SN_WORLD_DECL / WorldDecl scaffolding stays    │
     │ unused (optional later cleanup).                                        │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Verification                                                            │
     │                                                                         │
     │ make format, then the lit harness under tests/unit/language/ (with      │
     │ systems//pool tests):                                                   │
     │ - 1a import + size: plugin (shape + integrate :: sys, no pool) imported │
     │ by a driver writing lib.Particle[8], inserts, runs, reads back.         │
     │ Golden: one @lib.Particle global sized 8.                               │
     │ - 1a conflict diagnostic: two pool decls for one shape → new error      │
     │ (negative test).                                                        │
     │ - 1b literal == name: a program referencing a shape by name and by      │
     │ arche {foo,bar} → golden shows one shared %struct/global; IR identical  │
     │ to the name-only equivalent.                                            │
     │ - Doctest driver: doctest in the plugin file supplies its own pool/defs │
     │ and drives the system → arche test <plugin> passes; absent from a       │
     │ driver's arche build IR.                                                │
     │ - Bare unaffected: existing single-file Particle[N] program             │
     │ byte-identical. Regression gate tests/unit/language/systems/ green.     │
     │ - Part 2: verification defined in its follow-up detailing pass          │
     │ (notably: bar implements foo rebind produces correct shared canonical   │
     │ storage; unsatisfied requirement errors at the driver).                 │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Backward compatibility                                                  │
     │                                                                         │
     │ Part 1 is additive — bare Name[...] pool decls, single-file programs,   │
     │ and existing IR unchanged; new surface is an optional .-qualified pool  │
     │ name and functional anonymous arche {...} literals. Part 2 adds new     │
     │ region kinds (#requires/#implements) used by no existing program, and   │
     │ must keep single-module programs and tests/unit/language/systems/       │
     │ byte-identical.                                                         │

● Applied: the CLI is now arche init driver <plugin> / arche init plugin <name>,
  and the binding clause takes a comma list so one driver type implements many 
  plugins at once — Thing :: archetype { pos, bar } implements physics.Particle,
  render.Sprite.

  Now the discussion you asked for — #requires banner region (A) vs
  <module>.i.arche interface file (B). The subagent grounded both in the
  codebase:

  What pulls toward B (interface file):
  - Arche already encodes file role by name — core.arche is special-cased (by
  inode, compile.c:241), test_*.arche is a loose convention, and module
  discovery globs *.arche by extension via try_load_module_dir rather than
  parsing for role. So "filename carries role" is established precedent, not a
  new concept.
  - A module is already a folder of merged files; a multi-file plugin then has
  one canonical interface file instead of #requires scattered across files you'd
  have to grep.
  - O(1) discovery: arche init driver and IDEs find the contract by globbing
  *.i.arche, no parse. Enforces interface/impl separation by construction.

  What pulls toward A (#requires region):
  - Banners (#foreign/#module/#file, parser.c:901) are the established
  "everything is a region" idiom — your own stated mentality — and #requires
  reuses SN_REGION with near-zero new machinery.
  - The signal stays in-band and explicit: you see #requires in the source,
  rather than inferring a contract from a filename suffix.

  The hybrid (.i.arche file that's all #requires) is redundant — the filename
  already does the marking, so the banner adds nothing.

  The honest tension: A is more faithful to "everything is a region"; B is more 
  faithful to "everything is a group/folder-of-files" and is strictly better for
  tooling. The subagent recommends B, mainly on discoverability and the
  single-canonical-interface argument for multi-file plugins; I find that
  persuasive, but A is the more conservative, more architecturally-consistent
  move and avoids introducing a second filename-role convention beyond
  core/test_*.

  I've recorded this as an open decision in the plan (Part 2, leaning B, written
  to work under either). Which way do you want it — the in-band #requires
  region, or the <module>.i.arche file?
